    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="405237e501fb48e7988407077025769b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '811d896d-a10f-4a9b-91f2-09de045b12f8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d154261173" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion-batteries (SIB) are a low-cost alternative to currently used lithium-ion batteries (LIB) but suffer from poor cycling stability. Spray drying provides porous, hierarchically structured particles of cathode active material (CAM) in large amounts, suitable for up-scaling. Changing the chemical composition of the Na<sub>0.6</sub>Al<sub>0.11 − <i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>0.22 − <i>y</i></sub>Fe<sub><i>x</i> + <i>y</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>O<sub>2</sub> layered oxides under identical synthesis conditions lead to differences in particle morphology, conductivities, sodium vacancy ordering, and phase transition, therefore influencing the electrochemical performance via several mechanisms. Here, a broad overview on these changes for samples with variable nickel and iron content is presented. With increasing iron content, the particle porosity is reduced and lower initial capacity is received for most cycling windows. Substituting half of the original Ni amount with Fe still leads to high capacities and improved cycling stability. The influence of Al as electrochemically inactive element becomes visible in stabilized cycling stability as&nbsp;well.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202301854-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>More improvements in energy storage technologies are required to address global challenges such as the decrease of greenhouse gases. The electrification of the transport sector exhibits a potential of reducing emissions and large-scale stationary storage is regarded as a crucial technology to smooth the fluctuations of renewable energies in the power grid.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0001_R_d154261165e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - J. Energy Storage">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides high energy density and safety precautions, the cost of battery cells is a key factor in battery development.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0002_R_d154261165e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As we move toward sustainable energy, there is a rising need for energy storage technology. Vaalma et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0003_R_d154261165e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> forecast in their work from 2018 that the demand for lithium and cobalt induced by the global battery industry alone will climb to a level considerably beyond supply output in the following decades until 2050. This analysis shows that the price of either of the two elements, as well as any battery system based on them, will rise significantly over the next few decades. Sodium, magnesium, and aluminium are lithium-alternative elements for energy storage and are by three orders of magnitude more abundant in the earth's crust.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0004_R_d154261165e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Their energy storage mechanisms have already been investigated in battery systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0005_R_d154261165e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Sci. China Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0006_R_d154261165e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these post-lithium systems sodium-ion-batteries are the closest to commercialisation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0007_R_d154261165e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrated by various companies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0008_R_d154261165e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They will not completely replace LIBs, but rather more SIBs are thought to be a promising and cost-effective alternative battery technology. Moreover, it should be mentioned, that copper foil is not required for SIBs as a current collector. On both electrodes, aluminium, which is the third-most abundant material in the continental crust of the earth, can be employed as an inexpensive current collector.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0003_R_d154261165e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0005_R_d154261165e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Sci. China Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Layered oxides are a promising candidate for SIB cathode active material (CAM). They are known for their high specific capacity and high average operating voltage. However, capacity fading presents a problem for their application. Irreversible capacity loss can result from a variety of degradation mechanisms, such as severe volume change of the crystal structure, which can lead to loss of contact<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0009_R_d154261165e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and phase transitions at voltages beyond 4.1V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0010_R_d154261165e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These phase transitions may involve the oxidation of oxygen from the lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0011_R_d154261165e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The crystal structure consists of transition metal (TM) layers, just like their LIB counterparts. These layers are capable of intercalating Li and Na ions. Many TMs, including Mn, Fe, Co, and Ni, as well as combinations of them, have been studied and have an impact on the electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0012_R_d154261165e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Storage Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Two primary groups of the layered oxides that can be distinguished are P2-type and O3-type layered oxides, in which the environment of the sodium ion is either prismatic or octahedral. The number of non-equivalent TM layers are represented by the integers two and three.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0013_R_d154261165e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Typically temperatures between 650 and 900°C and a sodium content of 0.3 ⩽ <i>x</i> ⩽ 0.7 (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>) lead to the formation of P2-type layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0014_R_d154261165e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The lack of sodium in P2-type materials is a drawback, since it results in reduced capacity in full cells. Pre-sodiation of electrodes may be able to resolve this problem, but poses another challenge in the preparation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0015_R_d154261165e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There are two possible sites for Na ions in the prismatic (P-type) environment. Because of the large ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup>, adjacent prismatic Na<sub><i>e</i></sub> (edge sharing) and Na<sub><i>f</i></sub> (face sharing) sites cannot be occupied at the same time.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0012_R_d154261165e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Storage Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, sodium ion vacancies in the structure are created. An ordered intermediate phase results from ordering of these vacancies. Several voltage plateaus and diffusion barriers appear when this order is changed during charge and discharge. Absence of distinct plateaus suggests a solid-solution reaction free of a superstructure and therefore with lowered sodium diffusion&nbsp;barriers.</p>
         
         <p>Substitution of different TMs or electrochemically inactive metals such as Mg or Al into the layered structure can lead to a smoothing of these plateaus and can improve battery performance at higher C-rates,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0016_R_d154261165e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Sci. Adv">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as well as rate stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0017_R_d154261165e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Alloys Compd.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The right combination of different metals can be understood as engineering toward optimised properties. Fu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0018_R_d154261165e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrated that the use of electrochemically inactive Mg or Ca individually can improve the lattice stability of a P2-type Ni-Mn-based layered oxide. However, it was also observed that the Ca-substituted material showed unwanted side-reactions in the high voltage region. This disadvantage could be reduced when small amounts of magnesium were added to the chemical composition, demonstrating a synergetic effect of the Mg/Ca&nbsp;co-substitution.</p>
         
         <p>Cathode active materials with an engineered microstructure consisting of nanosized particles have been demonstrated to be an effective way to boost initial specific capacity and improve capacity retention after cycling in LIB and SIB systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0019_R_d154261165e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0020_R_d154261165e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ren et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0020_R_d154261165e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> created nanofiber SIB cathode material with the electrospinning method and achieved remarkable cycling stability over 10,000 cycles with high rate. However, the industrial application of electrospinning is limited due to the low production rate of material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0021_R_d154261165e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Wiley Interdiscip. Rev.: Nanomed. Nanobiotechnol.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another approach was chosen by Wagner et&nbsp;al.,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0019_R_d154261165e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> who proposed a spray drying technique to synthesise CAMs with a hierarchical structure of spherical (secondary) particles composed of nanosized (primary) particles. This spray drying technique produces micrometer sized CAM powders with a high tap density and is suitable for up-scaling toward industrial production.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0022_R_d154261165e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Materials">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In this work, we present the synthesis, structural and electrochemical characterization of four different P2-type cathode materials for SIBs with a hierarchical microstructure. Starting point is the nickel containing Na<sub>0.6</sub>Al<sub>0.11</sub>Ni<sub>0.22</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NANMO), inspired from the work of Hasa et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0023_R_d154261165e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, aluminium acts as inactive component for better cycling stability. Due to the toxicity of nickel and being a major cost driver for battery materials, a sustainable battery chemistry with this element is questionable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0002_R_d154261165e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0024_R_d154261165e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Basic Clin. Physiol. Pharmacol.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, nickel is gradually replaced with non-toxic and low cost iron to&nbsp;receive Na<sub>0.6</sub>Al<sub>0.11</sub>Ni<sub>0.11</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Fe<sub>0.11</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NANMFO) and Na<sub>0.6</sub>Al<sub>0.11</sub>Fe<sub>0.22</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NAFMO). Finally, the electrochemically inactive element aluminium is also substituted with iron, which yields a composition of Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMFO).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202301854-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301854-sec-0030-title">2.1 Chemical Composition and Particle Morphology</h3>
            
            <p>The obtained chemical composition was controlled after the final heat treatment with ICP-OES. Only less than 0.4 wt% of residual carbon remain after the final heat treatment with a sintering temperature <i>T</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> = 950°C in air atmosphere. The other elements are in good agreement with the nominal values and are summarized in the Supporting Information (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The primary particles of the synthesized CAM samples after the ball milling process show a broad size distribution between 0.2 and 10µ m as determined by laser light scattering. The average primary particle sizes are 1.67, 1.65, 1.42, and 1.11µ m for NANMO, NANMFO, NAFMO, and NMFO, respectively. After spray drying these primary particles are agglomerated into porous, spherical granules with sizes ranging between 3 and 30µ m. An overview of the particle sizes can be found in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The porosity of the granules lies between 53% and 60%, whereas a general trend can be observed, that the iron containing samples exhibit higher porosities. The BET specific surface area shows similar values for the four materials (12.3to 15.2m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) except for NMFO with a high value of 25.8m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. This high surface area and the smallest observed primary particle size for NMFO indicates that the ball milling process was most effective for this CAM. SEM micro graphs of the spray dried CAM granules are presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0001">1a</a>, which show the hierarchical micro-structure of the samples. The grain sizes seen by SEM are in the order of some hundred nano meters, which demonstrates that the average primary particles measured with light scattering are actually several agglomerated crystallites. Sintering at 950°C changes the micro-structure of the CAM granules. It can be seen, that the porosity, specific surface area, as well as the surface roughness have decreased during this heat treatment. The primary particles receive a flake-like shape and the secondary particles maintain their spherical appearance. The CAM with the highest nickel content exhibits the highest porosity and surface area, whereas with increasing iron content these values decrease from NANMO to NMFO (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0001">1b</a>). The high surface area of NMFO results in a high sinter activity and drives the densification during the calcination process, leading to very dense granules with a low porosity of only 18%. Compared to the iron free material NANMO the iron containing samples all show an increased densification behavior with lower porosity values of the spherical granules, which indicates that a higher iron content is beneficial for the sinter activity of these CAMs and that iron enhances grain growth, as can be seen from the SEM images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0001">1b</a>.</p>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301854-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7888be16-6ddc-4230-8f00-48a3cc09e97b/aenm202301854-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7888be16-6ddc-4230-8f00-48a3cc09e97b/aenm202301854-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fcd7e95c-b7d2-4f98-a44d-9ec631328aff/aenm202301854-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7888be16-6ddc-4230-8f00-48a3cc09e97b/aenm202301854-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301854-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301854" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Particle morpholgy: SEM micrographs reveal the morphology of the active material. The secondary particles are shown in (a) after spray drying and in (b) after sintering with <i>T</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> = 950°C. Particle porosity Φ in % and specific surface area <i>A</i><sub><i>BET</i></sub> in m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> supplement the SEM pictures.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0002">2</a> the micro structure inside the particles is revealed with SEM cross sections&nbsp;of cathodes.
It can be seen that the spherical secondary particles are porous, which allows an electrolyte penetration inside of all CAMs. The cross-section&nbsp;images are in good agreement with the porosity data of sintered secondary particles (see. Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information): Porosity Φ decreases from NANMO (highest nickel content) to NMFO (highest iron content), while the primary particle size is increasing. The reason for that is the above described sinter activity due to the presence of&nbsp;iron.</p>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301854-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9fd2f137-c3f5-417c-8197-d571026f7eb8/aenm202301854-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9fd2f137-c3f5-417c-8197-d571026f7eb8/aenm202301854-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4aae8aed-8d94-43d3-9c08-5ef0b4a57676/aenm202301854-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9fd2f137-c3f5-417c-8197-d571026f7eb8/aenm202301854-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301854-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301854" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrode cross section: Ion-etched cathodes reveal the inter granular porosity of secondary particles. As presented with Hg-porosimetry data in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) the porosity decreases from highest nickel content (NANMO) to highest iron content (NMFO).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301854-sec-0040-title">2.2 Ex Situ XRD</h3>
            
            <p>Ex situ XRD measurements were performed after the final synthesis with the sintering temperature of 950°C. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0003">3</a> shows the XRD patterns together with the Rietveld refinement results of the four different chemical compositions. To limit the number of refined parameters, stoichiometry constraints according to the ICP-OES results, i.e., the sum of the TMs fractions was set to 1 and the sodium fraction was 0.6, were used. All materials crystallize as layered oxides in the ideal P2 structure with hexagonal space group <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>. The refined lattice parameters for NANMO are <i>a</i> = 2.8631(4)Å and <i>c</i> = 11.2352(4)Å. The gradual substitution of large Fe<sup>3 +</sup> ions (radius 0.65Å) into the crystal structure leads to an increase of the lattice parameters with increasing Fe content in the order NANMFO (<i>a</i> = 2.8746(3)Å and <i>c</i> = 11.2587(3)Å), NAFMO (<i>a</i> = 2.8829(6)Å and <i>c</i> = 11.2843(4)Å) and NMFO (<i>a</i> = 2.9163(3)Å and <i>c</i> = 11.2982(2)Å). The diffraction profiles of all synthesized materials exhibit anisotropic, hkl-dependent peak broadening, i.e. the 10l reflections are broader than the 00l and hk0 reflections. This kind of microstructural induced peak broadening hints to the presence of stacking faults, which was also reported earlier for layered P2-type sodium ion CAMs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0025_R_d154261165e753" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0026_R_d154261165e753" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Environ. Sci.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, during Rietveld refinements, profile broadening was accounted for by the use of a crystallite size broadening function together with the phenomenological anisotropic strain broadening model of Stephens.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0027_R_d154261165e759" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Stephens model improved the fitting quality of the profile shapes, but some discrepancies still exist. The volume average column lengths were estimated based on a double Voigt approach as implemented in the Topas software. For NANMO this value was calculated to approximately 67Å. The domain sizes for the iron containing materials are ≈61Å for NANMFO, ≈43Å for NAFMO and ≈65Å for NFMO. The largest observed crystallite size for NMFO is in accordance with the SEM investigations and underlines the increased crystal growth as the iron content is&nbsp;increased.</p>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301854-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/18545606-8099-4750-b37d-1328f407add1/aenm202301854-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/18545606-8099-4750-b37d-1328f407add1/aenm202301854-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/295c83ce-1e77-42db-8b72-4304fb978569/aenm202301854-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/18545606-8099-4750-b37d-1328f407add1/aenm202301854-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301854-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301854" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">X-ray diffraction patterns and Rietveld refinement of a) NANMO, b) NANMFO, c) NAFMO, and d) NAFMO materials sintered at 950°C. NANMO and NANMFO contain small amounts of an impurity phase with hexagonal R3m structure (lower tics), whereas NMFO shows very weak reflections of a MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> spinel phase (lower tics).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In case of NANMO minor additional reflections were present in the diffraction pattern. These reflections can be indexed with the acentric hexagonal space group <i>R</i>3<i>m</i> derived from the AlCuO<sub>2</sub> structure<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0028_R_d154261165e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and correspond to an impurity phase, most likely an O3-type layered oxide. The specific chemical composition of the impurity is unknown. For NANMFO the same reflections with less intensity are visible, while NAFMO exhibits no additional reflections. With the presence of an impurity phase, the sintering temperature <i>T</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> = 950°C can be interpreted as non-optimal. More homogeneity of the desired P2-type phase can in general be achieved with higher sintering temperatures. It was described before that the sinter activity is increasing with the iron content. Since more iron leads to a better diffusion of sintering particles a better homogeneity and therefore less impurity can be expected. This was supported by further experiments, which will be published subsequently, that showed that the impurity phase vanished for NANMFO at higher sintering temperatures, while for NANMO it was almost not visible anymore. In the presented work <i>T</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> = 950°C was chosen as a compromise in order to compare all four materials after the same synthesis process. NMFO is nearly phase pure. Only very weak reflections of a different impurity phase isostructural to MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> spinel<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0029_R_d154261165e815" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Mater. Res. Bull.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are visible in the diffraction pattern. A summary of the refined crystallographic parameters can be found in the Supporting&nbsp;Information.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301854-sec-0050-title">2.3 Mößbauer Spectroscopy</h3>
            
            <p>The chemical compositions of the four cathode materials were chosen to investigate the influence of the substitution of Al and Ni for Fe on the materials' properties. Ni is considered to be a cost driver of battery materials<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0002_R_d154261165e829" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while Fe is one inexpensive option to replace it. In P2-type oxides, the process of Ni<sup>3 +</sup> / Ni<sup>4 +</sup> oxidation/reduction takes place at around 3.5 to 3.7 V (against Na|Na<sup>+</sup>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0023_R_d154261165e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0030_R_d154261165e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Fe<sup>3 +</sup>/Fe<sup>4 +</sup> oxidation/reduction occurs at approx. 4.0 to 4.1 V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0031_R_d154261165e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Chem.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is necessary to understand if and to what extend Fe is electrochemically active in these iron containing cathode materials in the different voltage regimes. Mößbauer spectroscopy was performed on the pristine materials after sintering at <i>T</i><sub>S</sub> = 950 °C, and after charging to 4.6&nbsp;V. For the materials recovered after charging, thick cathode sheets were used (coated with 800&nbsp;µm gap size, ca. four times higher mass loading compared to regular electrodes, for better signal intensity). When the upper voltage limit was reached, the cell voltage was kept constant until the current was smaller than C/50 to ensure full charge. The cells were then opened in argon atmosphere, the cathodes were washed with dimethylcarbonate (DMC), and removed from the current collector. About 50&nbsp;mg were recovered from two coin cells to achieve the desired sample&nbsp;mass.</p>
            
            <p>While the spectra of the pristine materials show exclusively Fe<sup>3 +</sup>, the spectra of the samples obtained after charging to 4.6&nbsp;V reveal a mixture of Fe<sup>3 +</sup> and Fe<sup>4 +</sup>, i.&nbsp;e., the partial oxidation of Fe<sup>3 +</sup> to Fe<sup>4 +</sup> during the charging process (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The fractions of Fe<sup>3 +</sup> and Fe<sup>4 +</sup> are determined from the area fractions of the corresponding doublets in the Mößbauer spectra and listed in in <b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#aenm202301854-tbl-0001">1</a>. The different samples show different degrees of Fe oxidation. A more effective Fe oxidation can certainly be achieved via higher upper cut-off voltage (but this might be detrimental to the electrochemical performance), lower charging current, thinner electrodes, higher porosity of secondary particles, and smaller primary particles. The lowest amount of Fe<sup>4 +</sup> was measured for NANMFO. This cathode material still contains Ni that contributes to the specific capacity. For that reason, a low amount of Fe<sup>4 +</sup> is explainable. In the case of the nickel-free materials NAFMO and NMFO, a significantly higher Fe<sup>4 +</sup> fraction was detected. For NAFMO the highest amount of Fe<sup>4 +</sup> was measured, since this active material has a higher porosity of 32% after sintering at <i>T</i><sub>S</sub> = 950 °C, while for NMFO it is only 18%. In the SEM micrographs of cathode cross-sections&nbsp;(shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0002">2</a>) it is also visible that NMFO exhibits bigger primary particles than NAFMO, which is also a reason for sluggish Fe<sup>3 +</sup> oxidation inside these particles. The before proposed mechanism between iron content and sinter activity results here in differently high amounts of Fe<sup>4 +</sup>.</p>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="aenm202301854-tbl-0001">

            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301854-sec-0060-title">2.4 Conductivity</h3>
            
            <p>In general, a cathode material needs good ionic and electronic conductivity σ<sub><i>j</i></sub> (<i>j</i> = i, e). These are fundamental and intrinsic properties of the cathode materia.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0032_R_d154261165e1031" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Only a few reports in the literature present intrinsic transport properties of active material without conductive carbon. The latter is added in the slurry preparation and improves the overall electronic conductivity of an electrode,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0033_R_d154261165e1036" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but here we are interested in the conductivities of the pure cathode active&nbsp;material.</p>
            
            <p>For NMC (lithium nickel-manganese-cobalt oxide) Zahnow et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0032_R_d154261165e1045" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> investigated both the electronic and ionic conductivity. They suggested a physically meaningful equivalent circuit where the contributions of the two resistors <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> (electronic) and <i>R</i><sub>i</sub> (ionic) to the impedance were overlapping. In case of sodium-based layered oxides, only few reports can be found in the literature, such as the work from Walczak et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0034_R_d154261165e1057" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Technol.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> where only the electronic conductivity was determined. In the present work, we suggest a combination of the two mentioned approaches (Walczak et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0034_R_d154261165e1062" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Technol.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Zahnow et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0032_R_d154261165e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>): An investigation of a sodium-based layered oxides with the analysis method from Zahnow et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0032_R_d154261165e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to determine the ionic part of the conductivity. However, the circuit model proposed by Zahnow et&nbsp;al. had to be modified to be applicable in current case of a layered Na transition metal&nbsp;oxide.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">A detailed description of the impedance spectra, the used equivalent circuit model, and its interpretation can be found in the Supporting Information. Performing these impedance measurements at various temperatures, one obtains the activation energies for the electronic and ionic contributions, i.&nbsp;e., plotting log (σ<sub><i>j</i></sub><i>T</i>) versus reciprocal temperature in accordance with the Arrhenius law (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202301854-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>)) leads to linear plots (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0004">4</a>).

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202301854-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/322cad26-33af-4419-a7f9-481ed44ad5a0/aenm202301854-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202301854:aenm202301854-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202301854-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>σ</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi>σ</mi>
                                    <mn>0</mn>
                                 </msub>
                                 <mi>T</mi>
                              </mfrac>
                              <mo>·</mo>
                              <mi>exp</mi>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                 <mo linebreak="badbreak">−</mo>
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>k</mi>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                       <mi>T</mi>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </mfenced>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} \sigma = \frac{\sigma _0}{T} \cdot \exp {\left(- \frac{E_{\rm A}}{k_{\rm B} T} \right)} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>As described in the previous sections, the following trend for the sintering activity was observed when comparing the four different chemical compositions. This activity increased when going from high nickel content to high iron content and <i>A</i><sub>BET</sub> and Φ were inversely proportional to this sintering activity. In case of the conductivities, such a trend is not visible. The conductivity is related to crystal structure and electronic band structure. Introducing substitutional elements may change the bandgap and therefore affect the electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0035_R_d154261165e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Ceram. Int.">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0036_R_d154261165e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ionic conductivity is influenced by the concentration of charge carriers, their mobility, and their charge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0037_R_d154261165e1135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Phys. Chem. B">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In <b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#aenm202301854-tbl-0002">2</a> the room temperature conductivities and activation energies <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9156b9a0-7647-463f-8442-75ce85c55522/aenm202301854-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202301854:aenm202301854-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202301854-math-0002.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>E</mi>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">j</mi>
                        </msub>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$E_{\mathrm{A_j}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> (<i>j</i> = e, i) for the transport mechanisms are summarized. The materials are sorted from highest Ni content to highest Fe content from top to&nbsp;bottom.</div>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301854-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3aa2115c-2d11-44e7-b21d-0b7525eab40b/aenm202301854-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3aa2115c-2d11-44e7-b21d-0b7525eab40b/aenm202301854-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d7c967b3-8c83-4390-b5c6-ea5b8098c2de/aenm202301854-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3aa2115c-2d11-44e7-b21d-0b7525eab40b/aenm202301854-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Arrhenius plots of a) electronic and b) ionic conductivity. The logarithm to the base ten was used for the plot for simple interpretation. Calculation of activation energies used natural logarithm.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="article-table-content" id="aenm202301854-tbl-0002">
