               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>It is clear that, while NMFO displays the lowest values for both electronic and ionic conductivity, NANMO exhibits the highest values for both. Although the porosities of NANMO and NAFMO are just slightly different, their electronic and ionic conductivities differ by one and two orders of magnitude, respectively. Although it can be assumed that some of the variation of σ<sub><i>j</i></sub> is caused by the various porosities, it is doubtful that this will cause the conductivity to alter by several orders of magnitude. Yet, while having different porosities, NANMFO and NAFMO show quite similar conductivities. So it can be assumed that the conductivities are not just a result of the samples' porosities but represent a material property. In a completely dense sintered sample without pores or grain boundaries the conductivities depend only on the chemical composition. It has been reported that the introduction of substitution ions (doping) can reduce more of the transition metal ions from their tetravalent state TM<sup>4 +</sup> into the trivalent state TM<sup>3 +</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0035_R_d154261165e1394" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Ceram. Int.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A mix of both valence states TM<sup>4 +</sup>/TM<sup>3 +</sup> can lead to the formation of electron holes in the narrow TM<sup>4 +</sup>/TM<sup>3 +</sup> band, which increases the conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0038_R_d154261165e1408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With Mößbauer spectroscopy it was demonstrated that exclusively Fe<sup>3 +</sup> was present in the iron containing CAMs. Unfortunately the same method could not be used to investigate iron free NANMO. It seems possible that Mn might exhibit a mix of valence states, since its transition of Mn<sup>3 +</sup> / Mn<sup>4 +</sup> in half cells is located close to the open circuit voltage (synonymous to the pristine material investigated with EIS).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0039_R_d154261165e1420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> That would also result in a mix of Ni<sup>2 +</sup> / Ni<sup>3 +</sup> as well. The incorporation of Fe<sup>3 +</sup> would shift those ratios. That might explain the high electronic conductivity of NANMO. An example of how the chemical composition can influence the valence states is given by Carlier et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0040_R_d154261165e1432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Chem.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> for a Li-based layered oxide. They reported that Co<sup>3 +</sup> is less likely to be oxidized to Co<sup>4 +</sup> if nickel is present in the structure. On the other hand the diameter of substitutional elements can influence the lattice parameters and hence ionic diffusion in the lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0035_R_d154261165e1442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Ceram. Int.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>There are only few reports in literature about the conductivities of layered sodium oxides with comparable composition. Similar impedance measurements were conducted by Walczak et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0034_R_d154261165e1449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Technol.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>&nbsp;on</p>
            
            <p>Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and on Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. They noticed compressed semicircles in the Nyquit plots similar to those observed for NMFO in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). However, they could not differentiate between ionic and electronic processes and derived their conductivities from the fit of two RQ-elements in series, which correspond to <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> according to Equation (<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>) or (<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>) (Supporting Information). The results published by Walczak et&nbsp;al. for the electronic activation energy <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f862815f-2cea-4203-a118-d9e4c349e1c9/aenm202301854-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202301854:aenm202301854-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202301854-math-0006.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>E</mi>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                        </msub>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$E_{\mathrm{A_e}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> of 0.39&nbsp;and 0.37&nbsp;eV are comparable to the values found for NMFO in the presented investigation as well as for the other chemical&nbsp;compositions.</p>
            
            <p>In Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#aenm202301854-tbl-0002">2</a> also room temperature conductivities are shown. In the work from Walczak et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0034_R_d154261165e1505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Technol.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the composition with the highest iron content Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> shows the lowest conductivity σ<sub>e</sub> = 5.78 · 10<sup>−8</sup> Scm<sup>-1</sup>. This is in agreement with our data with σ<sub>e</sub> = 2.8 · 10<sup>−8</sup> Scm<sup>-1</sup> (NMFO, highest iron content) to 6.4 · 10<sup>−7</sup> Scm<sup>-1</sup> (NANMO, no iron content). It should be mentioned that Walczak et&nbsp;al. used <i>T</i><sub>S</sub> = 800 and 900&nbsp;°C instead of 950&nbsp;°C employed in this study, which is certainly a reason for the different value for NMFO. This also supports the previous idea that the ideal sintering temperature for NMFO and NAFMO is lower than 950&nbsp;°C. Furthermore, Walczak et&nbsp;al. did not mention what pressing force was applied and what porosity the pellets exhibited after the sintering.</p>
            
            <p>The activation energy for ionic hopping <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/254d3583-e466-4b85-8dc6-ebc2423b9613/aenm202301854-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202301854:aenm202301854-math-0007" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202301854-math-0007.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>E</mi>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                        </msub>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$E_{\mathrm{A_i}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> and the ionic conductivity can be compared to the results for Li-NMC from Zahnow et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0032_R_d154261165e1554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They determined the activation energy for the ionic hopping process to be between 0.34&nbsp;and 0.59&nbsp;eV, which is nearly identical to their measured <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5bc4befb-1d11-4101-9465-67489d0b0229/aenm202301854-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202301854:aenm202301854-math-0008" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202301854-math-0008.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>E</mi>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                        </msub>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$E_{\mathrm{A_e}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>. The same seems to be true for the four-layered sodium oxides, where both processes have similar activation energies. Different sintering temperatures and porosities were investigated by Zahnow et&nbsp;al. The values most similar to those of our investigations are <i>T</i><sub>S</sub> = 950 °C and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/ee1fe638-c757-4131-a945-520ea921e394/aenm202301854-math-0009.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202301854:aenm202301854-math-0009" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202301854-math-0009.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">Φ</mi>
                        <mo>=</mo>
                        <mn>24</mn>
                        <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                        <mo>%</mo>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\Phi =24\,\%$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>. Lithium-based NMC-pellets with these parameters exhibited σ<sub>e</sub> = 2.5 · 10<sup>−8</sup> Scm<sup>-1</sup> and σ<sub>i</sub> = 4.9 · 10<sup>−8</sup> Scm<sup>-1</sup>, with both values in the same order of magnitude and σ<sub>e</sub> &lt; σ<sub>i</sub>. In case of the sodium-based layered oxides this is only the case for NANMO. The other iron containing materials share the common feature that the ionic conductivity is approx. one order of magnitude smaller than the electronic&nbsp;one.</p>
            
            <p>One feature of this data needs to be highlighted, because it will be relevant later again: Even though NANMO exhibits the highest ionic conductivity, the activation energy <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b57e718f-24a1-4b77-a2c6-a4c083604cb9/aenm202301854-math-0010.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202301854:aenm202301854-math-0010" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202301854-math-0010.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>E</mi>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                        </msub>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$E_{\mathrm{A_i}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> of the ionic hopping process is higher than for NANMFO and NAFMO. Especially the difference between NANMO and NANMFO will become visible later in the in situ XRD data, as well as during electrochemical cycling.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301854-sec-0070-title">2.5 Operando XRD</h3>
            
            <p>The thicker cathodes used for the operando XRD study were precycled with 1C and delivered approximately 78% to 88% of the specific capacity of thinner cathodes, which were used for the electrochemical cycling tests. The structural evolution during galvanostatic cycling of the different CAMs is depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0005">5</a>, where the evolution of the 002 diffraction peak of the initial P2 phase together with the corresponding voltage profiles between 1.5 and 4.6V are shown. The complete set of diffraction patterns collected during the operando experiments can be found in the supporting information in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). The initial P2 structures are preserved for all investigated materials over a large voltage range during the second charge process. During this desodiation process the 002 reflections shift toward smaller angles, which is consistent with an increase of the c lattice parameters.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0010_R_d154261165e1640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When higher potentials are reached, the intensity of the 002 P2 reflection for NANMO, NANMFO, and NMFO decreases and broad, diffuse reflections occur at the high angle side of these reflections. This is due to the onset of a phase transformation to a so called “Z” phase or OP4 structure, which is an intermediate state between the transition of P2 into O2.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0010_R_d154261165e1646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A detailed overview of the evolution from the P2 structure to the “Z” phase at the high voltage region for the different materials is given in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). In contrast, NAFMO shows no signs of such a phase transition in this experimental setup. To ensure that this unexpected behavior is a material effect, the measurement was repeated several times with different cells and cathode&nbsp;sheets.</p>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301854-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/78ef3bb4-6a7d-4849-be5a-8bb14cc4b67c/aenm202301854-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/78ef3bb4-6a7d-4849-be5a-8bb14cc4b67c/aenm202301854-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/902615e0-4c2d-44d7-8d50-61a3345b66c3/aenm202301854-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/78ef3bb4-6a7d-4849-be5a-8bb14cc4b67c/aenm202301854-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301854-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301854" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ XRD: Contour plot of the diffraction intensities measured during the 2nd cycle with C/10 of half-cells in the voltage window of 1.5 to 4.6V. The displayed 002 reflex is shifted during cycling due to change of lattice parameters. For all CAMs except NAFMO a phase transition is visible, most prominently seen with the intensity drop of the 002 reflex. The transition is not complete with the upper limit of 4.6V (marked with a red dashed line), The biphasic region is marked with dashed white lines. In case of NAFMO a leap in the discharge curve is visible at ca. 2.75V. This is most likely an artefact due to the reactive sodium metal anode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0006">6</a> the change of the lattice parameters of the P2 structures of the different CAMs during (de-)sodiation is shown. It needs to be noted that some small deviations from the lattice parameters calculated from the ex situ XRD data of the pristine materials are visible. This might be due to precycling with 1C (necessary for cyclable thick electrodes) and/or be of electrochemical nature, since cell assembly itself can introduce structural change as reported in literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0041_R_d154261165e1680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Power Sources">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lattice parameter <i>a</i> changes almost linearly for NAFMO, but for the other three materials where a phase transition occurs at high voltages, a broad minimum is visible. The lattice parameter <i>a</i> corresponds to the in-plane TM-TM-distance and the lattice parameter <i>c</i> corresponds to the layer to layer distance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0010_R_d154261165e1692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During charging sodium ions are removed from between the layers. Lattice parameter <i>c</i> and the layer to layer distance increase due to the repulsive force between the layers, which were screened before by Na<sup>+</sup>. The increase of <i>c</i> stops shortly before the phase transition. Afterwards a drop of <i>c</i> in the high-voltage region can be seen. This drop shows different magnitudes depending on the cathode material. The drop in <i>c</i> arises probably from a similar effect like it is described for Li-based layered oxides in literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0042_R_d154261165e1708" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Phys. Chem. C">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The value of the <i>c</i> parameter is a combination of the heights of the Li-O and the TM-O slab. Kondrakov et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0042_R_d154261165e1716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Phys. Chem. C">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> explained the increase and drop of <i>c</i> during charging as follows: At the beginning the TM-O length is decreasing while the length of Li-O is increasing. In combination this leads to an increase in <i>c</i> because the magnitude of the Li-O change is larger. When the Li amount is low, both heights decrease at the same time, which becomes visible as a sudden drop in <i>c</i>. In case of NANMO and NANMFO a similar change in lattice parameters magnitude was observed. The different behavior of <i>c</i> at high voltage might be due to the oxidation of Fe<sup>3 +</sup> to Fe<sup>4 +</sup>, which takes place at higher voltages than Ni<sup>3 +</sup>/Ni<sup>4 +</sup> in P2 layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0031_R_d154261165e1738" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Chem.">31</a>, <a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0039_R_d154261165e1738" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For NAFMO a moderate change of lattice parameter <i>a</i> together with the smallest change in <i>c</i> can be observed, which is related to the absence of a phase transition. If the charge-process was not complete for NAFMO, then this might be a reason for the absence. However, the measurement was repeated four times with different cathodes and electrode sheets. A phase transition was never visible. On top of that, we can refer to the Mößbauer spectroscopy. It revealed an oxidation of approximately 37% iron into the tetravalent state after charging. This amount was similar, but even higher than for NMFO with only 32%. This confirms that the NAFMO cathode material can be charged far enough for a possible high voltage phase transition, if there is any. A possible source of error in this experimental setup might also be the thickness of the cathode. In order to receive sufficient signal from the scattered x-rays the cathodes for the operando measurement were prepared with higher loading of active material (see Experimental Section). This resulted in approx. 80% of accessible capacity compared to thinner cathodes used for the electrochemical tests. However, all materials were treated in the same way and all showed this reduced capacity to similar extent. Therefore, it can be proclaimed that a possible phase transition for NAFMO might occur but is delayed much further into the high voltage region, while the other materials have already developed a transition. Recent work about layered sodium oxides has shown, that the introduction of many different elements into the crystal framework has improved cycling and structural stability, sometimes inhibiting the phase transition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0043_R_d154261165e1748" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">43</a>, <a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0044_R_d154261165e1748" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Energy Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0018_R_d154261165e1753" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrated an synergetic effect when substituting with Ca and Mg at the same time. All this indicates that the design of the right chemical composition may cause less or a late phase transition, such as it is seemingly the case for&nbsp;NAFMO.</p>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301854-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/52415c04-3a35-4524-a307-a11be800d743/aenm202301854-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/52415c04-3a35-4524-a307-a11be800d743/aenm202301854-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f6a3d8ff-c69b-4a85-bada-26729410675c/aenm202301854-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/52415c04-3a35-4524-a307-a11be800d743/aenm202301854-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301854-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301854" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Refined parameters: The <i>a</i>-parameter is shown on the left-hand side and the <i>c</i>-parameter on the right-hand side, with the cell potential in red. During charging a contraction of the lattice along the <i>a</i>- and expansion along the <i>c</i>-axis can be seen (vice versa during discharging). Beyond 4.3V, the appearance of a new phase is marked as biphasic region. In case of NAFMO a leap in the discharge curve is visible at ca. 2.75V. This is most likely an artefact due to the reactive sodium metal anode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In case of NMFO the strongest change in both <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> was observed. This certainly has an influence on cycling stability (see later in electrochemistry) and demonstrates how aluminium doping (in case of NAFMO) improves the structure stability and hence the cycling behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0017_R_d154261165e1792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Alloys Compd.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A significant amount of lattice parameter change already occurred before the biphasic region, so even without the phase transition a difference between NMFO and NAFMO can be observed. In general, it can be seen, that the biphasic region is not centered at the point of maximum voltage. This might be due to several reasons like slow sodium ion kinetics,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0045_R_d154261165e1797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the thick electrode, or the current of C/10. A test with smaller current of e.g., C/20 and thinner cathodes might confirm&nbsp;that.</p>
            
            <p>In P2-type layered oxides sodium ions can occupy two different prismatic sites within the structure, Na<sub><i>e</i></sub> and Na<sub><i>f</i></sub>, that share edges or faces. Neighboring sites cannot be occupied at the same time, which leads to vacancies and an ordering of these. The reordering of vacancies creates diffusional barriers with certain activation energies. Less ordering can be achieved with substitution elements such as Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0016_R_d154261165e1812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Sci. Adv">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In case of lithium, it is difficult to observe such occupancies due to the low electron density of Li and therefore less interaction with X-ray beam. Sodium is heavier, contains more electrons and hence more interaction with the X-ray beam leading to more reliable data about the occupancies. Therefore, we attempted a Rietveld refinement of the operando data including the sodium ion occupancies of the P2 structure within the one-phase region. It should be noted here, that the refined occupation values may deviate from the actual, absolute values due to the limited quality of the operando XRD data, but the qualitative trends should be meaningful. The refined occupancies are presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0007">7</a>.
All materials exhibit higher occupancies for the Na<sub><i>e</i></sub> sites compared to the Na<sub><i>f</i></sub> sites. However, the refined values for both sodium sites are lower compared to the ex situ XRD refinements. The reason for that is likely to be the same as before in case of the lattice parameters (structural change introduced due to cell assembly<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0041_R_d154261165e1829" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Power Sources">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and precycling with 1C).</p>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301854-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3af10bce-6fa3-4444-b1a4-0ae35dd2fe01/aenm202301854-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3af10bce-6fa3-4444-b1a4-0ae35dd2fe01/aenm202301854-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ce97ad4b-6c0e-485d-a745-f5e9f5808bc6/aenm202301854-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3af10bce-6fa3-4444-b1a4-0ae35dd2fe01/aenm202301854-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301854-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301854" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium occupancy: Refined sodium ion occupancies for Na<sub><i>e</i></sub> and Na<sub><i>f</i></sub> within the P2-type structure. The chemical composition changes from high nickel to high iron content: NANMO (a), NANMFO (b), NAFMO (c) and NMFO (d). No unambiguous refinement was possible in the biphasic region. Sodium ions are removed from the layers during charging and both sodium sites get depleted (vice versa for discharging). In case of NAFMO a leap in the discharge curve is visible at ca. 2.75V. This is most likely an artefact due to the reactive sodium metal anode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>For NANMO the occupancy of Na<sub><i>e</i></sub> is slightly decreasing while that of Na<sub><i>f</i></sub> is decreasing much faster with a drop to zero. The same behavior was observed vice versa for discharging, when the sodium sites are refilled. This tells us that the depletion and filling of Na<sub><i>f</i></sub> is favored over Na<sub><i>e</i></sub>. In case of NANMFO and NAFMO a similar trend for both sites in filling and depletion is visible, so neither of the two sites appears to be favored over the other during charge and discharge. For NMFO a steeper slope for Na<sub><i>e</i></sub> than for Na<sub><i>f</i></sub> is visible. In this case Na<sub><i>e</i></sub> is favored over Na<sub><i>f</i></sub> during charge and discharge (vice versa to NANMO), but less distinctive as for NANMO. This effect can best be understood when comparing NANMO and NANMFO: Less vacancy ordering can be achieved when a substitutional element is introduced into the crystal framework.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0016_R_d154261165e1888" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Sci. Adv">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Less vacancy ordering (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0007">7b</a>) leads to lower activation energy barriers for the ionic hopping process. This lower ionic activation energy <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/927cbda6-c4be-4482-9c82-d347aff2aada/aenm202301854-math-0011.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202301854:aenm202301854-math-0011" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202301854-math-0011.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>E</mi>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>A</mi>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                        </msub>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$E_{A_i}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> was also found with impedance spectroscopy, when comparing the Arrhenius plots of NANMO and NANMFO shown above (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0004">4</a>). With this property there is no preferred sodium site for depletion and refilling, which results in the absence of distinctive voltage plateaus in case of NANMFO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0016_R_d154261165e1910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Sci. Adv">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In total, the reduced vacancy ordering is the reason for enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, seen as a smaller slope in the Arrhenius plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0004">4</a>), which does not necessarily imply a higher conductivity. When comparing the electrochemical performance of half-cells with NANMO and NANMFO it can be seen, that the reduced vacancy ordering of NANMFO improves the rate-capability for moderate and high C-rates (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0009">9</a>). A comparison of the electrochemistry of these two CAMs is particularly possible since both active materials exhibit quite similar morphologies (porosity and specific surface area). In case of NAFMO there is also a less distinctive voltage plateau visible and no preferred sodium site. NMFO exhibits similar less distinctive voltage plateaus but here a slight preference of Na<sub><i>e</i></sub> was observed. In conclusion it can be said that the sodium vacancy ordering depends strongly on the chosen chemical composition and was reduced in this investigation when iron is&nbsp;present.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301854-sec-0080-title">2.6 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>Different open circuit voltages (OCV) were observed for the different cathode materials in half-cells: 2.75, 2.69, 2.70, and 2.69V for NANMO, NANMFO, NAFMO, and NMFO, respectively. In <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0008">8</a> the charge–discharge-curves of the first two cycles of a rate-test with half-cells are shown (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) displays the curves of the other rates in the following rate-test). For NANMO clear voltage plateaus are visible (a). When half of the nickel content is substituted with iron to NANMFO the plateaus are less pronounced (b). This corresponds to the reduced sodium vacancy ordering as described before in the section&nbsp;about operando XRD. In case of NAFMO (c) and NMFO (d) plateaus are visible, but are less distinctive than for NANMO. In the first charge cycle the received capacity corresponds to the sodium amount in the structure (measured with ICP-OES, see Supporting Information). A contribution of sodium is possible due to the oxidation of the transition metals in the layered oxide. It can be calculated that at least for NANMO and NANMFO also oxygen oxidation O<sup>2 −</sup>/(O<sub>2</sub>)<sup><i>n</i> −</sup> must have been involved to achieve the specific capacity in the first charge cycle. This indicates that during the electrochemical test a phase transition involving oxygen oxidation, takes place, as it was found before with operando XRD. Therefore electrochemical cycling was performed with three different upper voltage limits 4.6, 4.3, and 4.0V in order to step-wise reduce cycling in this critical high voltage region. The influence on capacity, rate-capability and cycling-stability will be individually discussed in the following. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0008">8</a> it can be seen that in the second cycle the specific capacity exceeds the theoretical one, due to additional sodium from the Na metal anode. P2-type structures are sodium deficient: Na<sub>0.6</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (TM = transition metal). Only 0.6 sodium equivalent per formula are available. Cycling below the OCV in half-cells adds more sodium. This effect increases capacity, but is not accessible in full cells without&nbsp;pre-sodiation.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301854-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/191135c6-128e-48bb-a58d-77dc93ffdcaf/aenm202301854-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/191135c6-128e-48bb-a58d-77dc93ffdcaf/aenm202301854-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e7dbefab-9329-47b5-87bb-cfe28c118a74/aenm202301854-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/191135c6-128e-48bb-a58d-77dc93ffdcaf/aenm202301854-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301854-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301854" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Charge-Discharge-Curves: Displayed are first cycle with C/20 and the second cycle with C/10 of the four different CAMs. The plots show the capacity gain in the 2nd cycle from the sodium anode. The different materials in (a–d) exhibit altered shape of redox plateaus.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The average voltage (AV) during discharge is decreasing with the iron content. The measured average voltages between 1.5 and 4.6V in the discharge cycle with a C/20 rate are <i>AV</i><sub><i>NANMO</i></sub> = 2.96V, <i>AV</i><sub><i>NANMFO</i></sub> = 2.88V, <i>AV</i><sub><i>NAFMO</i></sub> = 2.80V and <i>AV</i><sub><i>NMFO</i></sub> = 2.7V. This decrease in the average voltage originates from the different voltage profiles shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0008">8</a>. For NANMO distinctive voltage plateaus at high voltages are visible, resulting in a higher average voltage. In case of NANMFO these high voltage plateaus are smoothed out and therefore a smaller AV is measured. Increasing the iron amount further (NAFMO and NMFO) leads to an almost linear slope of the discharge-curve and shifts them into the low voltage area. In such cases, a discharge plateau is not visible at high voltage, only one at approx. 2.0V.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0090">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="aenm202301854-sec-0090-title">2.6.1 Rate-Capability</h4>
               
               <p>A rate test followed by a long-term cycling test are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0009">9</a>. The cells were cycled with different upper cut-off limits: a) 4.6V, b) 4.3V, and c) 4.0V. Lower limit was always 1.5V. In general nickel containing materials (NANMO and NANMFO) exhibit higher initial specific capacities. With 4.6V as upper limit (UL) NANMO and NANMFO are almost identical in performance. In case of 4.3V as upper limit the best performance was observed for NANMFO. With 4.0V as upper limit NANMO and NANMFO perform again almost identical. Nickel containing materials show in general higher capacity. An overview of the rate-capability is given in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), where the relative discharge capacity as a function of the C-rate is shown. The charge–discharge curves during the rate-test are displayed in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information). It can be seen that for the iron containing materials an increase of iron leads to a decrease in the rate-capability. This correlates with the increasing primary particle size due to increased sinter activity and corresponding longer diffusion paths within the particles (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0002">2</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The size of primary particles has a strong influence on the rate-capability. Especially in combination with the diffusivity in the active material the influence of the primary particle size differs. While for materials with a high diffusivity the size of the primary particles has no influence on the rate capability, for low diffusion constants, the particle size plays an important role for the rate-capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0046_R_d154261165e2034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Batteries &amp; Supercaps">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In general the rate performance of NANMFO and NAFMO is quite similar due to small differences in the micro structure, whereas the large particles sizes of NMFO result the worst&nbsp;rate-capability.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301854-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9335fdea-703b-4c66-916a-2fdbe5945b97/aenm202301854-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/9335fdea-703b-4c66-916a-2fdbe5945b97/aenm202301854-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/621d534d-183e-482f-8d4f-189135980a78/aenm202301854-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9335fdea-703b-4c66-916a-2fdbe5945b97/aenm202301854-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301854-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301854" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Half-cell cycling: All four CAMs sintered at <i>T</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> = 950°C. Cycling vs. Na-anode with different upper limits: a) 4.6V, b) 4.3V, and c) 4.0V. Rate-test at the beginning and long term cycling with 1C. The corresponding charge–discharge-curves are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0008">8</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information).</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>The course of the C-rate dependence differs significantly for NANMO from that of the Fe-containing materials. While at low C-rates the drop in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information) is still relatively strong and comparable to NMFO, the drop is smaller at high C-rates. The reason for this is, on the one hand, the small primary particle size and, on the other hand, the one order of magnitude higher electronic conductivity as determined by EIS. Naumann et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0046_R_d154261165e2078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Batteries &amp; Supercaps">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have demonstrated how electronic conductivity in the material influences the rate capability as a function of porosity and secondary particle size. At good electronic conductivities, the particle morphology is not limiting, only at lower conductivities do the differences in microstructure or secondary particle morphology become noticeable. Thus, with the same microstructure/porosity or secondary particle size but different electronic conductivities, the rate capability can vary greatly. This is evident here when comparing NANMO and NANMFO, which have comparable particle properties but different electronic conductivities, and the better conductivity of NANMO results in better relative C-rate&nbsp;capability.</p>
               
               <p>Related to the ionic part of conductivity is the aspect of sodium vacancy ordering as described before, which should also be mentioned in this discussion. NANMFO is slightly more stable at moderate and high C-rates during rate-test (exception at 10C and with UL = 4.6V). With 4.3V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0009">9b</a>) as upper limit NANMFO deliverers more capacity for moderate and high C-rates as compared to NANMO. This is related to less vacancy ordering in case of NANMFO (see operando XRD data in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0007">7</a>). Less ordering lowers diffusion barriers for sodium transport which is best seen at moderate and high C-rates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0016_R_d154261165e2092" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Sci. Adv">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Before, with the upper limit 4.6V, this was only slightly visible, since the strong degradation for all materials is obscuring the effect. It is also worth mentioning that for NANMFO the phase transition starts at a higher cell voltage (as seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0005">5</a>).</p>
               
               <p>With UL = 4.0V NANMO and NANMFO are almost identical, just for moderate to high rates slightly better performance of NANMFO is observed, most probably due to the same vacancy ordering&nbsp;effect.</p>
               
               <p>For NAFMO a similar effect of less vacancy ordering was observed with in operando XRD and is visible in the windows 1.5 to 4.3V and 1.5 to 4.0V, although it is less distinct. At small rates NANMO exhibits higher specific capacity (due to its advantageous particle morphology), but with moderate rates (1C – 5C) the difference between NANMO and NAFMO becomes smaller, due to the absence of vacancy ordering in&nbsp;NAFMO.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0100">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="aenm202301854-sec-0100-title">2.6.2 Cycling Stability</h4>
               
               <p><b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#aenm202301854-tbl-0003">3</a> summarizes the electrochemical performance in terms of discharge capacities in some specific cycles for all cathode materials and for the three cycling windows. An upper cut-off limit of 4.6V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0009">9a</a>) results in a strong degradation, which can be ascribed to the occurrence of phase transitions in the high voltage region beyond 4.0V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0023_R_d154261165e2121" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Operando XRD (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0005">5</a>) has revealed that all CAMs but NAFMO exhibit a phase transition in this region. Even without an observed phase transition NAFMO shows degradation. One reason for that is severe change in the lattice parameters <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0006">6</a>). Another reason might be, that in case of NAFMO a phase transition might be delayed to higher voltages or higher sodium amounts to be extracted. The latter could occur with thinner cathodes, such as used for the electrochemical cycling presented in this section&nbsp;(see Experimental Section). Reducing or avoiding the phase transition and lattice stress by lowering the upper limit to 4.3 and 4.0V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0009">9b,c</a>) improves long term stability. With Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#aenm202301854-tbl-0003">3</a> it can be seen, that the retention is similar for the individual materials in windows 1.5 to 4.6V and 1.5 to 4.0V, with 71% and 89% average retention. However, it can be noted that in cycle 39. (begin of long-term cycling with 1C) the nickel containing materials exhibit higher capacities than the&nbsp;others.</p>
               <div class="article-table-content" id="aenm202301854-tbl-0003">
                  
                  
                  
                  
                  <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table  3.
                    </span>Summary of electrochemical performance: Data taken from Figure&nbsp;<span class="section-footNote"><a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0009">9</a></span>. Specific capacities from cycle 1, 39, and 100. The capacity retention obtained with different upper voltage limits can be compared in the last row. The retention refers to the last 61 cycles with 1C.</header>

                  </div>
                  
                  
                  <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
               </div>
               
               <p>In the window 1.5 to 4.3V NANMFO and NAFMO exhibit a better stability than NANMO and NMFO. There are several possible reasons for this behavior. From Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5.12</a> (Supporting Information) it is evident that polarization of the cells is increasing for all materials, especially for NMFO. Another reason for the different long-term stability of the four materials might be that the biphasic region for NANMFO starts at higher voltage than for NANMO (see in situ XRD in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301854-fig-0005">5</a>). Therefore, when an upper limit of 4.6V is used in the cycling tests, both materials exhibit a phase transformation to a Z-phase, which results in a similar degradation behavior. Lowering the cut-off value to 4.3V prevents this phase transition for NANMFO, whereas NANMO still undergoes this transition. This reduces the material degradation for NANMFO, while at the same time relative high capacities are&nbsp;maintained.</p>
               
               <p>Further reducing the cut-off voltage down to 4.0V leads to an improved stability for all materials due to avoidance of severe structural changes and phase transitions. In the voltage window 1.5 to 4.0V the iron containing materials (NAFMO, NMFO) show better long-term cycling stability than nickel containing materials (NANMO, NANMFO). The reason for that is unclear. The long-term stability of NAFMO and NMFO is almost identical, which means that aluminium substitution for NAFMO has no effect on cycling-stability when cycled only up to 4.0V. The beneficial role of aluminium becomes apparent at higher voltages but there is a trade off against the lower specific&nbsp;capacity.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0110">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202301854-sec-0110-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we presented a two-step spray-drying synthesis process, which can be easily adapted for the production of larger amounts of cathode active materials for SIBs. We synthesised four different P2-type cathode materials consisting of hierarchically structured particles. The nickel contains layered oxide Na<sub>0.6</sub>Al<sub>0.11</sub>Ni<sub>0.22</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NANMO) serves as a reference material. The gradual substitution of nickel with environmental benign and cost-effective iron yielded the other investigated materials: Na<sub>0.6</sub>Al<sub>0.11</sub>Ni<sub>0.11</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Fe<sub>0.11</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NANMFO), Na<sub>0.6</sub>Al<sub>0.11</sub>Fe<sub>0.22</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NAFMO) and Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMFO). The micro structure of the hierarchically structured particles, particularly porosities and specific surface area, depend strongly on chemical composition. When substituting Ni with Fe, the porosity and surface area decrease. This effect is caused by the higher sinter activity when the iron content is increased, which also results in an increased grain growth. NMFO shows the largest grain sizes and the lowest internal porosity within the granules. Therefore, this material exhibits a low rate-capability and the lowest specific capacity. Partial substitution of iron with aluminium (NAFMO) seems to suppress the phase transition on deep charge at high voltages, which demonstrates the beneficial role of aluminium doping for the structural stability of Na-battery cathodes. Of these four layered oxide materials, the most promising is NANMFO, which has a high initial capacity and good capacity retention in each voltage window. The properties in the voltage window from 1.5 to 4.3V are noteworthy: NANMFO shows a very good initial capacity of 189mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at C/20 and 148mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even at 1C. The capacity retention after 100 cycles exceeds that of the other materials with 86.5%. Even at 10C this material shows a capacity retention of 50%. The potential of this material class can only be exploited, if the structural prerequisites for good electrochemical performance are met by the chemical composition. This is made possible by suitable synthesis conditions and thus an ideal micro structure. The material design must be adapted to the intended utilisation profile of such sodium-ion-battery cells, whereby limits are set with regard to the usable voltage window.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0120">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202301854-sec-0120-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0130">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0140">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301854-sec-0150-title"> Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The following reactants were used for the precursor preparation: Sodium acetate anhydrous (99% Merck), Aluminium nitrate nonahydrate (98% Acros Organics), Nickel(II) acetate tetrahydrate (99% Aldrich Chemistry), Iron(III) nitrate nonahydrate (98% Alfa Aesar), Manganese(II) acetate tetrahydrate (99% Aldrich Chemistry). The chemicals were dissolved in the right ratio for the different materials in different containers in deionized water and were stirred overnight. The solutions were combined and stirred for another hour, followed by spray drying (Mobile Minor, GEA) with an inlet temperature of 200°C and an outlet temperature of 108°C (batch size approx. 200 to 600g). The obtained precursor powders underwent a two-step heat treatment. First at 1000°C in air to remove the organics of the reactants. Grinding in a Pulverisette five planetary mill (Fritsch) with 3mm ZrO<sub>2</sub> milling balls in deionized water for 24h (planetary disk at 206rpm, bowl at 447rpm) resulted in an aqueous suspension of fine primary particles. This suspension was spray dried again with the same parameters as before into a fine powder of secondary particles (batch size approx. 50 to 100g). The spray dried powders were finally calcined at 950°C in air with a dwell time of 6h and heating rate of 5°C min<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301854-sec-0160-title"> Material Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>A Supra 55 (Zeiss) scanning electron microscope (SEM) was used to visualize the morphology of the spray dried particles. The specific surface area was investigated with a Gemini VII 2390a (NSA, Micromeritics) and calculated with the Brunauer-Emmet-Teller (BET) method. Porosity of the secondary particles was measured with mercury intrusion with a CEI Pascal 1.05 (Thermo Electron). To control the chemical composition of the cathode materials, inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) was performed (iCAP 7600 Duo, Thermo Fisher Scientific). The carbon content was measured with a CS-analyser and oxygen with carrier gas hot extraction (TGHE). The particle size distribution was measured via laser diffraction (Horiba LA950, Retsch Technology). Mößbauer spectroscopy was used to investigate the Fe<sup>3 +</sup>/Fe<sup>4 +</sup> ratio of the iron containing samples in their pristine state and after being fully charged. <sup>57</sup>Fe Mößbauer spectroscopy was performed at room temperature on a WissEL constant-acceleration spectrometer in transmission mode with a <sup>57</sup>Co(Rh) source. X-ray diffraction (XRD) was performed ex situ on a D8-Advance (Bruker) with Cu <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/83577c42-1e30-47d2-a9c3-667ddaaf127a/aenm202301854-math-0012.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202301854:aenm202301854-math-0012" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202301854-math-0012.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>K</mi>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>α</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo>,</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msub>
                           </msub>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$K_{\alpha _{1,2}}$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math> radiation (mean wavelength λ = 1.54184Å) and a Lynxeye XE-T&nbsp;detector.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301854-sec-0170-title"> Electrochemical Experiments</h4>
                  
                  <p>Electrode preparation was done by mixing the cathode powder with an N-Methyl-2-pyrrolidon (NMP) based slurry with polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF, Sigma–Aldrich) and the conductive carbon TIMCAL-C65 in a mass ratio of 80:10:10 in a SpeedMixer DAC 150 (Hauschild). The mixture was coated on aluminium foil with the doctor blade method, a gap size of 200µ m and a speed of 1.5mms<sup>-1</sup> on the device Coatmaster 510 (Erichsen) and dried with 120°C under vacuum over night. Average active material mass loading of prepared cathode sheets was 3.3mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Cathodes with a diameter of 12mm were punched and pressed with a force of 5kN. Half cells were built as coin cells CR2032 with the cathodes, 16mm diameter glass fibre separator (Whatman GF/C, 260µ m thickness), a 12mm sodium metal anode. As electrolyte were used 100µL of Propylene Carbonate (PC) with 1M NaPF<sub>6</sub> and 5% Fluoroethylene (FEC). Galvanostatic cycling with potential limitation was performed on an Arbin potentiostat in the electrochemical window of 1.5 to 4.0 / 4.3 / 4.6 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. The current of 1C for cycling was defined according to Hasa et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0023_R_d154261165e2702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> based on the theoretical capacity with 0.6 Na of the pristine structure, which resulted in the currents of <i>I</i><sub><i>NANMO</i></sub> = 164mA g<sup>−1</sup>, <i>I</i><sub><i>NANMFO</i></sub> = 165mA g<sup>−1</sup>, <i>I</i><sub><i>NAFMO</i></sub> = 165mA g<sup>−1</sup> and <i>I</i><sub><i>NMFO</i></sub> = 159mA g<sup>−1</sup>. To make sure the discharge step starts with a fully desodiated cathode the charge current was kept at small values during the rate-test. For small discharge rates, such as C/20 and C/10 the same rate was used for charging and for discharge rates beyond C/2 the current for charging was kept at C/2. At the beginning of a electrochemical cycling formation cycle with 1C was performed (not shown).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0180">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301854-sec-0180-title"> Electrochemical Experiments—Impedance</h4>
                  
                  <p>Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed on pure cathode material without any binder or conductive carbon. The powders received after the second spray drying process (before second heat treatment) were pressed with a PW10 manual laboratory press with a force of 20kN and a press tool with a diameter of 12mm into pellets. The resulting thickness depends on the used powder and varies between 0.9 and 1.2mm. The pellets were sintered at 950°C for 6h. Afterward they were dry smoothed with sand paper (grit size 1000) and sputtered with gold on both sides. To improve the connection in silver ink was added to both sides on top. Vacuum drying at 120°C was done to remove any moisture and the pellets were finally assembled into Swagelok cells under an argon atmosphere. For the EIS measurements a VSP-300 (BioLogic) potentiostat was used with 50mV amplitude in the range from 7MHz to 10mHz. The cells were kept in a BTZ-175 (ESPECS) climate chamber. Temperature was set in 5 °C steps between to 60 °C. Before measurement the system waited 0.5h to 1h until the temperature chamber was stable and another waiting period of 1h was implemented to make sure, the Swagelok cells had adopted the&nbsp;temperature.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301854-sec-0190">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301854-sec-0190-title"> Electrochemical Experiments—Operando X-Ray Diffraction</h4>
                  
                  <p>For operando XRD measurements a laboratory diffractometer for battery research with Mo-<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d337c21c-a106-4a8c-95b1-7df728e0e2b3/aenm202301854-math-0013.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202301854:aenm202301854-math-0013" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202301854-math-0013.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>K</mi>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>α</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msub>
                           </msub>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$K_{\alpha _{1/2}}$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math> radiation was used.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301854-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301854-bib-0047_R_d154261165e2763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To increase the intensity thicker cathodes were prepared as described above but with a gap size at the doctor blade of 400µ m, which resulted in a mass loading of 8.0 to 9.5mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Coin cells with polyimide windows were prepared and after a formation cycle the current during XRD measurement was C/10. Acquisition time for each diffraction pattern was 10 min which corresponds to Δ<i>x</i> ≈ 0.01 in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>. Rietveld refinement was executed with the software Topas&nbsp;V6.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
